Calibration: The sensor is calibrated using standard solutions of quinine at known concentrations. The electrochemical response (such as current or potential change) is measured and plotted to generate a calibration curve, establishing the relationship between quinine concentration and sensor output.\n\n2. Selectivity: The sensor's response to quinine is tested in the presence of potential interfering substances (e.g., other alkaloids, ions, or common matrix components) to ensure that the IDA mechanism is specific to quinine.\n\n3. Sensitivity and Detection Limit: The lowest concentration of quinine that can be reliably detected (limit of detection, LOD) and quantified (limit of quantification, LOQ) are determined. This is crucial for assessing the sensor's practical utility.\n\n4. Reproducibility and Stability: The sensor's performance is evaluated over multiple measurements and over time to ensure consistent results. This may involve repeated assays with the same and different sensor units.\n\n5. Recovery and Accuracy: The sensor is tested with real or spiked samples (e.g., beverages or biological fluids) to assess recovery rates and accuracy compared to known concentrations.\n\n6. Comparison with Reference Methods: Results from the electrochemical IDA sensor are compared with established analytical techniques for quinine detection, such as HPLC or spectrophotometry, to validate accuracy and reliability.\n\nIn the context of IDA, the verification also involves confirming that the indicator displacement mechanism is functioning as intended: the indicator (often a redox-active or electroactive molecule) is initially bound to a receptor, and upon addition of quinine, the indicator is displaced, leading to a measurable electrochemical signal. Control experiments without quinine and with structurally similar compounds help confirm the specificity of the displacement event.\n\nOverall, a combination of analytical validation (calibration, Calibration: The sensor is calibrated using standard solutions of quinine at known concentrations. The electrochemical response (such as current or potential change) is measured and plotted to generate a calibration curve, establishing the relationship between quinine concentration and sensor output.\n\n2. Selectivity: The sensor's response to quinine is tested in the presence of potential interfering substances (e.g., other alkaloids, ions, or common matrix components) to ensure that the IDA mechanism is specific to quinine.\n\n3. Sensitivity and Detection Limit: The lowest concentration of quinine that can be reliably detected (limit of detection, LOD) and quantified (limit of quantification, LOQ) are determined. This is crucial for assessing the sensor's practical utility.\n\n4. Reproducibility and Stability: The sensor's performance is evaluated over multiple measurements and over time to ensure consistent results. This may involve repeated assays with the same and different sensor units.\n\n5. Recovery and Accuracy: The sensor is tested with real or spiked samples (e.g., beverages or biological fluids) to assess recovery rates and accuracy compared to known concentrations.\n\n6. Comparison with Reference Methods: Results from the electrochemical IDA sensor are compared with established analytical techniques for quinine detection, such as HPLC or spectrophotometry, to validate accuracy and reliability.\n\nIn the context of IDA, the verification also involves confirming that the indicator displacement mechanism is functioning as intended: the indicator (often a redox-active or electroactive molecule) is initially bound to a receptor, and upon addition of quinine, the indicator is displaced, leading to a measurable electrochemical signal. Control experiments without quinine and with structurally similar compounds help confirm the specificity of the displacement event.\n\nOverall, a combination of analytical validation (calibration,
